NATIONAL BUREAU OF STANDARDS
The National Bureau of Standards¹ was established by an act of Congress March 3,
1901. The Bureau's overall goal is to strengthen and advance the Nation's science and
technology and facilitate their effective application for public benefit. To this end, the
Bureau conducts research and provides: (1) a basis for the Nation's physical measure-
ment system, (2) scientific and technological services for industry and government, (3)
a technical basis for equity in trade, and (4) technical services to promote public safety.
The Bureau consists of the Institute for Basic Standards, the Institute for Materials
Research, the Institute for Applied Technology, the Center for Computer Sciences and
Technology, and the Office for Information Programs.
THE INSTITUTE FOR BASIC STANDARDS provides the central basis within the
United States of a complete and consistent system of physical measurement; coordinates
that system with measurement systems of other nations; and furnishes essential services
leading to accurate and uniform physical measurements throughout the Nation's scien-
tific community, industry, and commerce. The Institute consists of a Center for Radia-
tion Research, an Office of Measurement Services and the following divisions:

well-characterized materials needed by industry, commerce, educational institutions, and
Government; provides advisory and research services to other Government agencies;
and develops, produces, and distributes standard reference materials. The Institute con-
sists of the Office of Standard Reference Materials and the following divisions:
Analytical Chemistry-Polymers-Metallurgy-Inorganic Materials-Reactor
Radiation-Physical Chemistry.
THE INSTITUTE FOR APPLIED TECHNOLOGY provides technical services to pro-
mote the use of available technology and to facilitate technological innovation in indus-
try and Government; cooperates with public and private organizations leading to the
development of technological standards (including mandatory safety standards), codes
and methods of test; and provides technical advice and services to Government agencies
upon request. The Institute also monitors NBS engineering standards activities and
provides liaison between NBS and national and international engineering standards
bodies. The Institute consists of a Center for Building Technology and the following

ices-Housing Technology-Federal Building Technology-Structures, Mate-
rials and Life Safety-Building Environment-Technical Evaluation and
Application-Fire Technology.
THE INSTITUTE FOR COMPUTER SCIENCES AND TECHNOLOGY conducts re-
search and provides technical services designed to aid Government agencies in improv-
ing cost effectiveness in the conduct of their programs through the selection, acquisition,
and effective utilization of automatic data processing equipment; and serves as the prin-
cipal focus within the executive branch for the development of Federal standards for

To

iii
\n--- PAGE 00000006 ---\nAbstract
This book is a revised and vastly expanded version of the monograph, R. C.
Bose, W. H. Clatworthy, and S. S. Shrikhande, Tables of Partially Balanced De-
signs With Two Associate Classes, Tech. Bul. No. 107 of the North Carolina Agri-
cultural Experiment Station, Raleigh, North Carolina, 1954.
Approximately 950 combinatorially distinct designs are given, which repre-
sents a doubling of the number of parametric specifications for designs of the origi-
nal publication. The introduction includes special instructions to aid analysis using
hand calculations and a section on the general least-squares approach for use when
the analysis is to be done on an electronic computer.
For those interested in the construction of the designs, information is pro-
vided on methods of construction and extensive references, including original
sources for the designs, are given. A tabulation of known information about unique-
ness, resolvability and the nature of the corresponding dual and complementary
designs is also included for each design.
Key words: Analysis of PBIB designs; experimental designs; finite fields; finite
geometries; finite projective planes; incidence matrices; incomplete block designs;
mathematics; partially balanced; partially balanced incomplete block designs:
PBIB designs; statistics; tables of PBIB designs.
iv
\n--- PAGE 00000007 ---\nPreface
This volume is the result of cooperative efforts of several organizations and individuals. The
initial idea of revising the much referenced monograph of incomplete block designs,
R. C. Bose, W. H. Clatworthy, and S. S. Shrikhande,
Tables of Partially Balanced Designs With Two Associate Classes,
Tech. Bul. No. 107 of the North Carolina Agricultural Experiment
Station, Raleigh, North Carolina, 1954,
came after I received an invitation from Joan Raup Rosenblatt and Joseph M. Cameron to spend
a part of my 1968-69 sabbatical leave with the Statistical Engineering Laboratory of the National
Bureau of Standards. Since the establishment of its Statistical Engineering Laboratory in the mid-
Forties under the direction of Churchill Eisenhart, the Bureau of Standards has consistently pro-
vided strong support for the development of experimental arrangements useful in statistically
planned and analyzed experiments. As a former member of this Laboratory I had done much inves-
tigation into extending the number of available partially balanced designs of the type catalogued in
the publication of Bose, Clatworthy and Shrikhande (hereafter briefly referred to as “BCS(1954)”).
It was natural then to conceive of a sabbatical year project which would combine mutual interests
of the writer and the National Bureau of Standards to provide a greatly expanded revision of
BCS(1954) incorporating the many published results of a host of researchers in partially balanced
designs. This group includes W. J. Youden, Joseph M. Cameron, William S. Connor, Dale M.
Mesner and myself, all former members of the Statistical Engineering Laboratory, as well as many
other investigators of the United States, Canada, England, India, Japan and China.
The project of revising BCS(1954) has been financially supported directly by my "home"
institution, the State University of New York at Buffalo, through sabbatical salary received during
Academic Year 1968-1969, through a Grant-in-Aid, Contract 50-1120-A, received from The Re-
search Foundation of State University of New York, and through a National Science Foundation
Grant, GP8391, which supported me during the summers of 1968, 1969, and 1970, and completed
my financial support during the other half of Academic Year 1968-69.
I was also generously assisted in many ways by the University of North Carolina where I
worked full time on this project during AY 1968-69 as Visiting Professor with the Department
of Statistics. At Chapel Hill I was provided office space, secretarial assistance, use of library facili-
ties, and the congenial professional association so necessary to an extended project of this
magnitude.
I am deeply indebted also to the Department of Experimental Statistics and to the North
Carolina Agricultural Experimentation Station of the State University of North Carolina for their
approval to conduct the revision of BCS(1954).
The National Bureau of Standards has assisted the writer in so many ways in the preparation,
editing, and publication of this volume that any attempt to enumerate them fully will be futile.
I spent parts of the summers of 1968, 1969, and 1970 as a Guest Worker and have been in constant
communication with associates there during the Academic Years from September 1968 through
June 1971.
To Joseph M. Cameron of the Office of Measurement Services I am indebted for much profes-
sional advice, for helpful suggestions in the early stages of preparation, and for providing a general
least-squares approach to the analysis of the designs which is included in Chapter 3 of the
Introduction.
\n--- PAGE 00000008 ---\nTo Joan Raup Rosenblatt, Chief of the Statistical Engineering Laboratory, I am grateful for
provision of congenial and delightful working conditions, and for giving me access to the Bureau's
staff and facilities.
To Janace Speckman of the Statistical Engineering Laboratory I am deeply grateful for as-
sistance extending over the 3-year period. About 95 percent of the statistical experimental arrange-
ments of this volume have been checked for their validity by computer techniques devised and
programmed by Janace Speckman and Joseph M. Cameron. All but minor parts of all tabulations
of this publication were typeset through computer-controlled processes. This necessitated key
punching of most of the contents of the odd numbered Tables V through XIX onto computer cards
and subsequent verification through a series of intricate arithmetical and combinatorial checks.
The tabular portions of this publication evolved from the author's handwritten copy. The transition
from handwritten copy to computer output has required of Miss Speckman extraordinary alertness
to detect errors and many forms of inconsistency in writing. Her critical reading of drafts of the
introductory chapters led to a number of improvements.
The vast amount of key punching required was done by Mrs. Eleanor S. Brown and Miss
Joanne Mobley.
Mrs. Carla G. Messina of the Office of Standard Reference Data deserves special mention for
devising the computer program for transforming ordinary computer output into the phototypeset
pages.
The introductory Chapters I and II, parts of Chapter III and the bibliography were typed in
final manuscript by Veronica Connor of the Statistical Engineering Laboratory. For her stenographic
skills and daily assistance with many requests I am most grateful.
Because of the rather extensive nature of BCS(1954) and its widespread use among researchers
in combinatorial arrangements and by experimentalists, it is felt that a listing of the main new fea-
tures of this volume would be informative. This is provided in the following section.
What is new in this volume. The heavy dependence of this volume upon the original publica-
tion will be obvious to anyone familiar with it. Insofar as the economic aspects of publication would
permit, the strong features of the BCS(1954) publication have been retained. For those familiar with
partially balanced designs it is perhaps worthwhile to summarize the new features of this volume.
Among them the following are perhaps worth mentioning:
1. The number of parametric specifications for designs has been increased from the 376 cata-
logued by BCS(1954) to 781 in this volume. The BCS(1954) publication contained a single design
construction for each parametric specification; in the present volume many parametric specifica-
tions have multiple constructions, providing altogether approximately 950 combinatorially distinct
designs.
2. Table 0 is an auxiliary table which provides the user of this volume a quick summary of
those designs of interest to him based on his requirements as to the number v of treatments, the
number k of experimental units per block and the number r of replications of each treatment.
3. For all designs whose plans were explicitly presented, a computer verification was per-
formed to show that the design is a valid one having the specified association scheme. This made
possible the detection and correction of the few cases of incorrect designs in BCS(1954). Approxi-
mately 95 percent of the designs of this volume have been computer verified for validity.
4. Standardization of association schemes has been provided for nearly all designs that have
specific displayed plans or constructions. These association schemes are displayed in Table II and
then referred to by symbolic designators whenever a plan is displayed. This standardization of asso-
ciation schemes has made necessary the reorganization of the solutions or plans of many previously
published designs.
5. Standardized sets of blocks constituting complete replications of treatments have been in-
troduced as construction modules or units (to reduce publication costs). These are displayed com-
pletely in Table III and then referred to by symbolic designators in presenting the plans of certain
regular group divisible, semi-regular group divisible and Latin square type designs.
vi
\n--- PAGE 00000009 ---\n6. The computational constants C1, C2, H, and ▲ and the efficiency factors E1, E2, and E, which
were computed by hand for BCS(1954), have been computer evaluated for this volume.
7. The literature sources for all designs published herein have been provided in the "even
numbered" Tables VI through XVIII. Whenever published plans for the same parametric specifi-
cation have been found to be combinatorially equivalent, this has been indicated along with an at-
tempt to identify the earliest publication.
8. Wherever possible, an attempt has been made to determine whether a parametric specifi-
cation for a design has the possibility of multiple combinatorially distinct plans. Cases known to
possess unique solutions (up to an isomorphism) are identified. This is the first time this has been
attempted in a large scale enumeration of partially balanced designs.
9. For each specifically displayed plan for a design an attempt has been made to provide infor-
mation regarding the corresponding complementary and dual designs. The resolvability status of
each plan is also specified whenever it is known. (This last is new only for designs not previously
published in BCS(1954).)
10. Each of the "even" numbered Tables IV through XVIII contains for each design an entry
which specifies the required number (vr) of experimental units as a ready reference in assessing the
sizes of the tabled designs.
11. Chapter 3 contains a special section, contributed by Joseph M. Cameron and Janace
Speckman, on the general least squares approach for use when the analysis is to be done on an
electronic computer. The same chapter contains a new section which emphasizes the convenience
and simplification in data analysis that arises from use of the association schemes and the recorded
computational constants C1, C2, H, and ▲, when desk calculators are employed.
The accuracy of the uniqueness and dual design assertions described in items 8 and 9 is not
amenable to computer verification. Responsibility for their validity is fully my own. The incom-
pleteness of this information is an indication that there is yet much to learn about the structural
nature of incomplete block designs.
WILLARD H. CLATWORTHY
February 20, 1971

\n--- PAGE 00000012 ---\n\n--- PAGE 00000013 ---\nChapter I. Introduction to the Tables
1. History. Incomplete block designs have become highly developed as statistical tools for the planning,
conduct, analysis, and interpretation of scientific experimentation conducted in laboratories and test instal-
lations where a high degree of experimental control is desirable or even necessary to cope with the experi-
mental variability that invariably arises throughout the conduct of the experiment. Historically the statistical
use of these designs began with the balanced incomplete block designs introduced by Yates (1936a) and de-
veloped by Fisher and Yates (1938) and Bose (1939) and extensively tabled in Fisher and Yates (1963) and
Cochran and Cox (1957) along with the lattice designs of Yates (1936b, 1937) and Cochran and Cox (1957)
all of which were developed for use in agricultural and biological experiments. Further refinements in sta-
tistically based experimental arrangements of the first type were developed by Youden (1937, 1951) and of
the second by Harshbarger (1946, 1947) and Nair (1951). Bose and Nair (1939) developed a very general
class of incomplete block designs which they called partially balanced incomplete block designs with m
associate classes (abbreviated PBIBD(m)). These designs include the balanced incomplete block designs
(BIB designs) and the square and rectangular lattice designs as special cases. Generally, they have the
advantage of providing an extremely large selection of experimental arrangements from which investigators
may choose the ones best suited to their needs. Besides the advantage of control of experimental variation
through the blocking technique, they offer (1) the opportunity to select plans of smaller size [fewer repetitions
of the treatments, varieties, test conditions, etc.], (2) statistical analysis of the experimental data by rela-
tively simple computational procedures, (3) simplicity of presentation and interpretation of the results from
comparative experiments in terms of clear-cut statements about their precision based on modern statistical
developments.
In the United States and England during the period of World War II, a strong interest was developed in
industry and in government to adapt the developments in probability and small sample theory to the control
of quality of production processes of industry. This interest was extended after the war to the introduction of
statistical procedures to the experimental problems of the physical and engineering sciences. This effort has
been particularly significant in the vast and expensive investigative activity associated with atomic power and
aerospace developments. Today there is intense interest in current developments in probability and statistics
for possible application to research and development in all branches of science, engineering, health and
medical research, business, and in fact, in any area where decisions need be made on the basis of incom-
plete information. Thus the incomplete block designs have potential for application to many fields of
investigation.
By 1952 R. C. Bose and a group of his advanced degree students at the University of North Carolina
(including S. S. Shrikhande, W. S. Connor, T. Shimamoto, and W. H. Clatworthy) had sufficiently extended
and developed the partially balanced incomplete block designs with two associate classes [PBIBD(2), or even
more briefly "D(2)" or "D(2) designs"] that several hundred were now available. With the encouragement of
Gertrude Cox, Director of the Institute of Statistics, Consolidated University of North Carolina, and with the
support of the Agricultural Experiment Station, North Carolina State College, some 375 of these D(2) designs
were classified, catalogued and generally put into convenient form for use by experimenters. A monograph
was published by Bose, Clatworthy and Shrikhande (1954) very soon after Bose and Shimamoto (1952) had
developed the concept of association schemes, on which the classification of D(2) designs is based. Since
1952 the concept of association schemes has been the subject of intensive research by statisticians interested
in the combinatorial problems of the design of experiments. The intimate relations between association
schemes and certain kinds of graphs has attracted many mathematicians interested in graph theory to join
in their investigation. The resulting improved insight into the construction problems of D(2) designs ac-
companying the improved knowledge of association schemes, led to the construction of literally hundreds
1
\n--- PAGE 00000014 ---\nof new designs of D(2) type since the publication by Bose, Clatworthy, and Shrikhande [hereafter frequently
referred to briefly as "BCS (1954)"].
The extremely widespread and frequent referencing of BCS (1954) by scholars all over the world, the
vast development of new designs of D(2) type, and the very high potential for use of these combinatorial
arrangements in scientific experimentation led the present writer to the decision to prepare an up-to-date
catalog of D(2) designs along with associated, but incomplete, auxiliary information heretofore unpublished
(in fact never before attempted for a large collection of designs). These special features are described in
sections 4 and 5 of this Chapter.
2. Definition of a partially balanced incomplete block design with two associate classes and the relations
between its parameters. Following BCS (1954), an incomplete block design is said to be partially balanced
with two associate classes if it satisfies the following requirements.
(i) The experimental material is divided into b blocks of k units each, different treatments being applied
to the units in the same block.
(ii) There are v ( > k) treatments each of which occurs in r blocks.
(iii) There can be established a relation of association between any two treatments satisfying the
following requirements:
(a) Two treatments are either first associates or second associates.
(b) Each treatment has exactly n ith associates (i = 1, 2).
(c) Given any two treatments which are ith associates, the number of treatments common to the
jth associate of the first and the kth associate of the second is p and is independent of the
pair of treatments we start with. Also pj = på¿¡(i,j,k−1, 2).
(iv) Two treatments which are ith associates occur together in exactly λ; blocks (i = 1,2).
For a proper partially balanced incomplete block design λ₁ # λ2. If λ₁ = λ2 or if one of the n; vanishes,
the design becomes a balanced incomplete block design.
The numbers v, r, k, b, ni, n₂, A1, and A2 are called the parameters of the first kind, whereas the num-
bers pik (i, j, k=1, 2) are called the parameters of the second kind.
It is to be noted that the association relations between the treatments of a partially balanced design
are governed solely by the requirements of paragraph (iii) of the definition and do not depend upon how
the treatments are distributed in blocks. The association scheme depends only on the parameters n₁, n2,
and pk (i, j, k=1, 2).

3. Classification of D(2) designs. This volume contains more than 800 experimental designs of type
D(2) for which 2 ≤ k ≤ 10 and 2 <r< 10 where k is the number of experimental units in a block and r is
the number of blocks in which each treatment appears. These designs are classified into the following
types:
(a) Group Divisible (GD), consisting of three subtypes
(al) Singular (S)
(a2) Semi-Regular (SR)
(a3) Regular (R)
(b) Triangular (T)
(c) Latin Square types (LS)
(d) Cyclic (C)
(e) Partial Geometry (PG)
(f) Miscellaneous (M).
These classifications are defined and briefly described in the next chapter. General least squares
analysis of D(2) designs is provided in the third chapter along with a general method of solution suitable for
modern computers. Methods for use with a desk calculator are also illustrated.
4. Scope of the tables. The extensive expansion of the present classification of D(2) designs from 375
to more than 800 parametric specifications necessitated the introduction of new modes of presentation to
conserve space and to minimize publication costs. Simultaneously, it has been attempted to provide con-
siderable auxiliary information not heretofore provided in such compilations of designs. To efficiently use
this volume the reader should be aware of the tabular subdivisions, their purpose and scope. There are 20
tables designated by 0 and the roman numerals I, II, . . ., XIX.
Table 0. Auxiliary Table for Identifying Designs of Potential Interest According to the Magnitudes
of Parameters k and v.
Table 0 is the key guide to entry of Tables IV through XIX. It is felt that the user of this volume, when he
is searching for a design, will generally know the block size k and the number v of treatments for his pro-
posed experiment. Thus Table 0 has 9 subheadings "k=2", "k=3”, . . ., “k= 10". Under each subheading
the entries are for increasing magnitudes of v, the number of treatments. Under k, v combinations, the entries
are for the number of replications r and the number b of blocks. The table entries are design reference
numbers having the combination of values k, v, r, b. Knowing these design reference numbers and the titles
for Tables IV through XIX, the user is guided to designs of interest. For instance, if the user is searching
for an experimental arrangement with blocks of size k=4 to accommodate v = 10 treatments and is willing
to have an r as large as 10, entry of Table 0 first for k=4, then v = 10 leads to the following designs for r=2,
4, 6, 8, and 10:

only the triangular design T30 available; but for r=8 and 10, there are five choices each, as indicated above.
The user is then led to one or more tables among Tables IV through XIX depending upon his choices.
Table I. Glossary and Notation. This table explains the concisely presented information in the tables.
Abbreviations and symbols are explained, as are technical terms. Terminology not explained in the intro-
ductory chapters is generally found here.

angular designs, Latin square type designs, and cyclic designs have been standardized. These association
schemes are referred to by symbols in Tables V, VII, IX, XI, XIII, and XV where the plans for the above
types are detailed. Thus the standardized association scheme for a GD design having m=2 groups and
n = 4 treatments per group is referred to by the symbol "(2,4)" opposite "Groups" in the Tables V, VII and
IX and reference to section A of Table II informs us that the standardized association scheme (2,4) is

Table III. Sets of Blocks Constituting Replications for Partially Balanced Designs Based on Group
Divisible and Latin Square Types of Association Schemes.
It was found that the plans for D(2) designs of GD and Latin square types sometimes could be expressed
in terms of sets of blocks, each constituting a complete replication of treatments and that these replications
were being repeated in many designs. This fact was utilized to reduce printing costs by displaying the replica-
tion sets in Table III and then referring to these in Tables VII, IX, and XIII.
The eight "even" numbered Tables IV, VI, . . ., XVIII provide a compact source of information for all
D(2) designs of the eight classifications. Each design has a reference number, such as S32 or LS 113, and
these occupy the left-most columns of the tables. Horizontal lines contain information about the design
whose reference number appears at the left.
For GD designs succeeding columns give the design parameters v, r, k, b, m, n, λ₁, and λ2; for the other
types the column headings are for v, r, k, b, n₁, n2, A1, A2. The next three columns are occupied by efficiency
factors E1, E2, and E which are computed from formulas given in chapter III. The next column contains the
number of experimental units in the design, N=vr=bk, and provides the user a quick index as to the size
of the design.
The next column heading is "Unique Solution" and the entry will be "Yes" meaning that the solution
is unique (up to an isomorphism), "No", meaning that two or more combinatorially distinct plans or solutions
are known to be existent, and these plans are very frequently presented in the following "odd" numbered
table; "?", meaning that the uniqueness status for this design specification is an unsettled question.
The next column is headed "Complementary Design." The design complementary to the given one will
be a design with the same number of treatments v and the same number b of blocks and its jth block will con-
sist precisely of those treatments which do not appear in the jth block (j = 1, 2, . . ., b) of the given design.
The new design has block size v-k and b-r replications. The entries in this column vary. If the complementary
design is small (i.e., has 10 or fewer replications) it will be identified specifically, as "Design SR6" for ex-
ample; whereas if it is large the entry may be "NL" followed by "r=xx" meaning the complementary
design is a design with the same association scheme, is not listed in this book and it has r=xx replications.
4
\n--- PAGE 00000017 ---\nIn recent years there has been some evidence that very large designs may become practical in instances
where huge numbers of observations are economically possible and are recorded by automatic recording
or sensing devices. Thus the column heading is intended as a constant reminder that there is a very large
supply of very large D(2) designs available and easily constructed from the plans that are provided in detail
in this volume. Other entries in this column are explained in Table I. Glossary and Notation.
For the Latin square types of designs only the next column heading is “L; (n)”, the entries being symbols
like L3 (5) or L (4) which identify the specific standardized association scheme being used.
The next column heading is "Dual Designs." The dual of a design D is a design D' whose treatments
are the blocks of design D and whose blocks are the treatments of design D. If design D has parameters
(v, b, r, k, . . .) then its dual D' has parameter (v', b', r', k', . . .) = (b, v, k, r, . . .). If the dual of
the particular solution or plan being referenced is known, it is identified by an entry, such as "Design SR28".
If the referenced design is symmetrical (i.e., has v=b) the entry may be "SD" (self dual) meaning that
the dual design has the same parameters as the referenced design, but that they may not be combinatorially
equivalent; or the entry may be "TSD" (truly self dual) meaning that the design is not only self dual but is
isomorphic with the referenced design. An entry "D(x)" where x is 3, 4, or 5 means that the dual of the
referenced design is a partially balanced design in the sense of Bose and Nair (1939) with x associate classes.
Frequently, the dual of a given design is not recognizable as any well known type, but one can say something
about what it is not. An entry "not a D(x), x = 1, 2, 3” means that the design is not a BIB design (not a
D(1)) and neither is it a partially balanced design with two or three associate classes. For entries that are
not explained here, the reader is referred to Table I. Glossary and Notation for an explanation.
The right-most heading is "Literature Sources", where the reader is referred to the references, which
are located after Table XIX. The reader is immediately impressed with the frequency of the reference
“BCS (1954)" as more than 350 of the designs of this tabulation are taken from this source. An intense
effort has been made to identify plans having the same parametric specification but combinatorially equiva-
lent plans. When such cases have been identified an indication of it has been made by first listing the first
publisher and in brackets listing the names of later writers who published combinatorially equivalent solu-
tions. For instance, the entry "P. M. Roy (1954) [Clatworthy (1956), John (1966)]" means that the solutions
published by Clatworthy (1956) and by John (1966), for the particular design being referenced, are combi-
natorially equivalent to the earlier solution by P. M. Roy (1954). For readers who have difficulty identifying
solutions as being the ones to which a source reference is given, there is the reminder that treatment symbols
were recast and plans rearranged frequently to put a plan into the standardized form of this volume. Also
the Literature Sources column is omitted from Table IV because, except for provision of multiple distinct
solutions for parametric specifications permitting nonunique solutions, the construction problem was
solved by Bose and his associates in 1952. The multiple solutions for SGD designs presented in this volume
were worked out by the author.
The "odd" numbered Tables V, VII, . . ., XIX provide detailed information about designs. For the GD
designs a referenced design is provided with the parametric specification

\n--- PAGE 00000018 ---\nentries are varied and were explained previously for Tables IV, VI, . . ., XVIII. Entries whose meanings

If the association scheme is not standardized it is either given in detail or else a design Reference No. is

(m, n), T(n), Li(n) or C(v) will be given to Table II where it is given in detail. The blocks themselves serve

last entry is "Plan." Here the plan may be given in detail as was always done in BCS (1954); a shortened form
using the replication sets of Table III may be employed; a shortened form using cyclical development of
specified initial blocks may be displayed; or a word message may be used (1) for convenience, (2) for designs
for which N=bk=vr is so large the design is thought to have slight usefulness or (3) for cases for which the
construction may be extremely tedious. The need to find ways to cut publication costs has affected the pres-
entation of the plans, but a side benefit may be greater accuracy in some instances.
5. Special features of these tables. This publication has a few features that are new for tabulations of
incomplete block designs of type D(2). Something in excess of 95 percent of the designs of this volume have
been tested by electronic computer for validity of the plan as a D(2) design with the specified parameters
and association scheme. The computational constants C1, C2, H, and ▲ and the efficiency factors E1, E2, and E
were computed according to the formulas of Chapter III. The only cases where designs were not computer
verified for validity were those for which the plans were described by word messages.

All the information that the reader now finds in the "odd” numbered Tables V, VII, . . XIX was trans-
ferred from the computer to magnetic tapes. These computer tapes became the input for programs developed
by the National Standard Reference Data System which essentially automatically controlled the type-
setting for these tables. Thus it is expected that a much smaller incidence of typographical errors will occur
in these particular tables.

\n--- PAGE 00000019 ---\nChapter II. The Classification of Designs
A. Group Divisible Designs
1. Introduction. The largest, simplest and perhaps most important class of D(2) designs is known as
group divisible. A group divisible (GD) design is a D(2) design for which the treatments may be divided
into m groups of n distinct treatments each such that treatments that belong to the same group are first
associates and two treatments that belong to different groups are second associates. The group divisible
association scheme is an mXn array of the treatment symbols (1, 2, mn=v, say) such that rows of
the array are groups.
(2.1)
For GD designs it is clear that
v = mn, n₁ =n-1, n₂ = n(m-1).

For any GD design λ2 > 0 is a necessary condition, since if λ2=0 then treatments that belong to dif-
ferent groups never occur together in a block and the design breaks up into m disconnected sets of blocks,
a degenerate form of design (called a disconnected design) that does not permit estimation of the comparisons
of treatments belonging to different groups. When the zero-valued λ in a group divisible design is λ2, it may
always be put into the proper form with A₁ = 0 by renaming the associate classes. (When properly renamed
it is always true that p₁₂ =0.)
2. Combinatorial properties of group divisible designs. Bose and Connor (1952) have made a careful
study of the combinatorial properties of group divisible designs, and classified them into three subtypes
depending on the values assumed by the quantities
Q=r-λ₁ and rk — vλ₂ = P.
They considered the v× b incidence matrix N= (nij) of a group divisible design, defined in the usual way
as in section 4 of this chapter, and showed that the determinant of the treatment structure matrix, NN', is
(2.4)

|NN' = rk(rk — vλ2) m-1 (r — λ₁) m(n−1)¸
They classified GD designs into three types from a consideration of the expression for | NN' | as follows:
(1) Singular (S) if r — λ₁ = 0,

-
(2a) Semi-regular (SR) if rk — vλ2 = 0
(2b) Regular (R) if rk — vλ2 >0.

(15) When in a SRGD design the number b of blocks assumes its smallest possible value, b=v-m+1,
any two blocks have k― (r− λ₁) treatments in common.
Definition. If we may separate the blocks of any design into r sets of t blocks each such that each set
contains exactly one replication of the treatments, the design is said to be resolvable. For resolvable designs
b=tr and v=tk. A set of blocks consisting of a complete replication of the treatments is called replication
group.
Results (16) and (18) are due to Bose and Connor (1952).
(16) For a resolvable semi-regular design b> v−m+r.
Definition. An affine resolvable design is a resolvable design for which any two blocks belonging to
different replication groups intersect in the same number of treatments.
(17) A SRGD design which is (1) resolvable and (2) has b-v-m+r is also affine resolvable (AR), and
conversely.
(18) For a regular GD design b≥ v and for a resolvable regular GD design b≥ v+r−1.
(19) For an affine resolvable semi-regular group divisible (ARSRGD) design, the common number of
treatments between any two blocks belonging to different replication groups is k/t and hence for ARSRGD
designs v must be divisible by t2.
(20) If the number b of blocks is even in an ARSRGD design, then (a) m must be a perfect square if
m and r are odd, while n must be a perfect square if m is odd and r is even.
(21) If the number b of blocks is odd in an ARSRGD design, then (a) c = k/m must be a perfect square if
m and r are odd; (b) nk must be a perfect square if m is odd and r is even; and (c) t must be a perfect square
if m is even and r is odd.
Remark. The results (20) and (21) arise from showing first that the quantity
t'nmkm(n-1)=v'nmkb-2r
must be a perfect square for ARSRGD designs.
(22) The necessary and sufficient condition for the existence in a SRGD design of two blocks such that
the number of treatments common to any other block and these two are equal, is that the two blocks contain
k-(r-λ) common treatments.

and if each group of treatments replacing treatment a of the BIB design is represented by a₁, α2,

In BCS (1954) one plan or solution is presented for each of the 124 possible parametric specifications
for the SGD designs of the subclass defined by r≤ 10. Due to the combinatorial uniqueness of the balanced
incomplete block [briefly D(1)] designs on which the SGD designs necessarily depend, there are 93 cases
for which the BCS (1954) plans are unique (up to an isomorphism) and hence completely solved. In the
present publication, Tables IV and V contain multiple plans or solutions for 20 of the 28 cases for which it
is known that multiple (combinatorially distinct) plans are possible. There are two cases for which uniqueness
is undecided in that the uniqueness status of the corresponding D(1) design is still unknown.
Table IV does not contain a "Literature Sources" section because the source is either BCS (1954) or
the additional (solutions) plans are given for the first time in this volume.
4. Construction of semi-regular group divisible designs. Bose, Shrikhande and Bhattacharyya (1953)
[hereafter briefly referred to as BSB (1953)] have made a far-reaching but not exhaustive study of the con-
struction of group divisible designs. Their treatment of the SRGD designs is highly systematic and utilizes
in many instances the combinatorial arrangements known as orthogonal arrays. Their work forms the basic
source for all SRGD designs given by BCS (1954), which included all parametric specifications, except for
one inadvertent omission, of the subclass of SRGD designs having 3 ≤ k ≤ 10 and 2 < r < 10. For these 91
cases BCS (1954) gave one solution or plan each. However, these designs are also capable of multiple solu-
tions in 22 instances and these are indicated in our Table VI and presented in our Table VII. It was deter-
mined that there are 28 parametric specifications for SRGD designs which give unique solutions including
the 17 cases for which the blocks are of size k=2. The latter designs were constructed by Clatworthy (1955)
who also provided new solutions for designs SR 24, 29, 51, 110. Other distinct plans were taken from publica-
tions by Bose and Nair (1939), Connor (1952), BSB (1953) [to replace the invalid solution for BCS (1954)
design SR 31], P. M. Roy (1954b, 1954c), John (1966), Clatworthy (1967) and adapted for the standardization
of association schemes and mode of display utilized in the present publication. There are 56 cases for which
the uniqueness status is unknown.
บ
Every blocked design has a treatment structure and a block structure. If we construct the v× b incidence
matrix N (ny), where
nij
=
if the treatment labeled i occurs in the block labeled j
0 otherwise
the v× v matrix NN' (N' being the transpose of N) provides a description of the treatment structure of the
design while the b×b matrix N'N provides a description of its block structure (i.e., tells how many treat-
ments every pair of blocks has in common). The design whose incidence matrix is N' is called the dual of
the design with incidence matrix N. The design with incidence matrix N' may not be any recognizable design.
If the design with incidence matrix N' has the same parameters as the design with incidence matrix N, we
the original design is self dual. If the two designs are also isomorphic we say the original design is truly
self dual. We might remark that there do exist cases of D(2) designs that are self dual but nonisomorphic.
say
The SRGD designs having r, k≤ 10 are strongly related through their duals to the balanced incomplete
block [D(1)] designs in some instances, to various D(2) designs based on Latin square types of association
11
\n--- PAGE 00000024 ---\nschemes in a great many cases, and to D(3) designs in several other cases. This class of designs is also
characterized by a high frequency of resolvability among the plans.
Two constructions of SRGD designs are given below, but generally the reader should consult BSB (1953).
4a. Construction of SRGD designs by "block cutting" of BIB designs. Suppose we start with a BIB
design D with parameters (v*, b*, r*, k*, λ*= 1). A particular treatment occurs in r* blocks. Suppose we
delete treatment 0 and the r* blocks in which it occurs from the design D.

Theorem: By omitting a particular treatment 0 and all the r* blocks containing it from a BIB design with
A*= 1, we obtain a GD design with the parameters given in (2.8).

\n--- PAGE 00000025 ---\n4b. Construction of SRGD designs by forming the dual of an affine resolvable BIB design. Consider an
ARBIB design with parameters (v*, b*, r*, k*, λ*). Recall that its blocks can be arranged in r* sets of t
blocks per set (b*=r*t) such that a set contains one exact replication of the treatments (so that v*= k*t).
Then since it is AR two blocks belonging to the same set intersect in µ₁ 0 common treatments while any
=
two blocks belonging to different sets intersect in µ2= k*2/v* common treatments. Hence, when we dualize
the BIB design, the t=b*/r* block symbols of a replication become the treatment symbols of a group and
these treatment symbols never occur together in any block of the dual, i.e., A₁ = µ₁ = 0 and л=t=b*/r*. On
the other hand, the block symbols for a pair of blocks belonging to different replications become second
associates in the dual and occur together as a treatment pair in λ2=µ2 blocks. Consequently, the dual is a
GD design with parameters

5. Construction of regular group divisible designs. BCS (1954) contains 68 constructions for the same
number of parametric specifications; these appear to depend heavily on BSB (1953) and upon the original
paper by Bose and Nair (1939). The BCS (1954) design designated R4 was found to be invalid but correctable
and their designs R56 and R60 contained typographical errors which were also corrected. The 68 designs
were also included in the present Tables VIII and IX. Besides these, other RGD designs were taken from the
literature and adapted to the present volume. Designs were taken from Bose and Nair (1939), Bannerjee
(1951), BSB (1953), P. M. Roy (1954c), Archbold (1955), Clatworthy (1955, 1956, 1967), Sprott (1955, 1959),
Archbold and Johnson (1956), Thompson (1956), Freeman (1957), Liu (1963), Liu and Chang (1964), David and
Wolock (1965), John (1966), Ikeda (1967, 1967b) and Masuyama (1967). Designs in this category appear to
be highly irregular in their block and treatment structures. Consequently, it does not seem likely that a few
general construction procedures are capable of yielding the plans provided here for 209 parametric speci-
fications having r, k≤ 10. The construction techniques employed in the literature display a wide spectrum
of ingenuity. This diversity may be explainable by the fact that the RGD designs are the only GD designs
for which the determinant |NN' | is positive.
Symmetrical regular group divisible designs very frequently have duals which are themselves RGD
designs with the same parameters, i.e., they are self-dual. Many such designs, both RGD and certain other
types of D(2) designs, have been identified by use of a theorem due to Hoffman (1963). However, in many
cases the author has been able to extend beyond Hoffman's "self-dual" conclusion to the stronger "truly
self-dual" conclusion. Entries to the "Dual Designs" columns of the "even" numbered Tables VIII, XII,
XIV, and XVIII and entries opposite "Dual” in the “odd” numbered Tables IX, XIII, XV, and XIX indicate
particular designs for which the above remarks apply.
Many published designs are isomorphic to others and we have attempted to give credit to the first
construction while indicating later isomorphic constructions by the author's name included in brackets
13
\n--- PAGE 00000026 ---\nwhen citing source references in Table VIII. Having thus thinned out the number of constructions, we
identified 39 cases of RGD designs whose parametric specifications possess nonunique solutions. There
are 58 identifiable cases for which the solutions are unique (up to an isomorphism); of these, 41 are cases
for which the design has block size k=2.
B. Triangular Designs
1. Definition. A partially balanced design with two associate classes is said to be triangular if the
number of treatments is v=n(n−1)/2 and the association scheme is an array of n rows and n columns with
the following properties:
(i) The positions in the principal diagonal (running from upper left-hand to lower right-hand corner) are
left blank.
(ii) The n(n-1)/2 positions above the principal diagonal are filled by the numbers 1, 2, . . .,n(n-1)/2
corresponding to the treatments.
(iii) The n(n-1)/2 positions below the principal diagonal are filled so that the array is symmetrical
about the principal diagonal.
(iv) For any treatment i the first associates are exactly those treatments which lie in the same rows
(or in the same columns) as i.
The following relations clearly hold:
(2.14)
v=n(n-1)/2,
n₁ =2(n-2),
n₂ = (n-2) (n-3)/2,

where n ≥ 5. (The case n = 4 has pi₂ =0 so that it is also of the group divisible type and is classified in this
way. We note further that for n=2, or 3, n₂ = 0 so that there is only one associate class and the design, if it
exists, degenerates to the D(1) design.)
We now state an important result which evolved from the research of Connor (1958), Shrikhande (1959a),
Chang (1959) and Hoffman (1960):
If an association scheme has parameters (2.14) and (2.15) and n=5, 6, 7 or n≥9 it is unique (up to an
isomorphism); if n=8 there are exactly four nonisomorphic association schemes.

The simple constructions given above provide quick constructions for some of the 100 parametric speci-
fications for triangular designs with r, k≤ 10 and listed in Tables X and XI. Other triangular designs, as
well as some of these simple ones, may also be constructed by mathematically sophisticated procedures.
Appropriate references to sources are given in Table X.
BCS (1954) provided 36 triangular designs having 3 ≤ k ≤ 10 and 2 < r < 10. Clatworthy (1956) was able
to expand the original listing to 76 designs by construction of new designs with k=2 and 3 and by including
n-plicates of solutions provided by BCS (1954). In the early 1960's three researchers at Peking University,
under the leadership of C. W. Liu, constructed many new triangular designs which have been included,
and Masuyama (1965) published new procedures for constructing triangular designs, several of which
were published the same year by the Chinese group. Shrikhande (1965), Ray-Chaudhuri (1962), (1965),
Seiden (1961), (1963) also constructed new triangular designs. Using a newly constructed D(1) design with
v=b=56,r=k=11 and λ=2 by Hall, Lane and Wales (1968), the author has constructed the new triangular
design T91.
It can be shown that 57 of the 100 parametric specifications for triangular designs given in Tables X
and XI lead to plans that are unique (up to an isomorphism). Of the remaining 43 parametric specifications
nonuniqueness has been established in 11 cases by the construction of 2 or 3 distinct plans for each.
Due to the fact that several of the triangular designs are of the linked blocks type, they are related to
balanced incomplete block designs through their duals, and consequently several others have duals which
are SGD designs. Among the triangular designs there exists ample evidence that a symmetrical triangular
design is not necessarily self-dual or truly self-dual as there are 5 cases for which the duals are SGD designs.
There are others which are truly self-dual and still others for which the weaker assertion, self-dual, can be
made.

1. Introduction. Consider a set of v=n2 treatments and an association scheme which consists of an
nxn array of the distinct treatment symbols 1, 2, . . ., v=n onto which a set of i-2 mutually orthogonal
Latin squares are superposed. Let the n, first associates of a treatment a consist of the n-1 other treat-
ments lying in the same row of the array with a, the set of n 1 other treatments in the same column with
-
a, and the n-1 other treatments corresponding to the same letter as a in each of the Latin squares. All
other treatments are second associates of a. Such an association scheme is called a Latin square type L₁(n)
association scheme.
A D(2) design based on a Latin square type Li(n) association scheme is called a Latin square type L₁(n)
design.
For these association schemes it is clear that

From these relations and the definition it is clear that certain restrictions on the parameters i and n
are necessary. First of all, for a given n the definition depends upon the existence of a set of mutually
orthogonal Latin squares so that for positive integral values of n for which a maximum number of squares
exist 0i-2<n-1 or 2 ≤ i ≤ n + 1. We note that when in+1, n₂ becomes 0, and any design based
on such an association scheme would necessarily degenerate to a D(1) design. If i=n then p₁₂=0 and the
design, if it exists, must be of GD type. To avoid both these possibilities we shall require i ≤ n−1. Clearly,
n> 3. Thus, the existence of a Latin square type Li(n) association scheme presupposes the existence of

When n=6, the only possible value for i is i=2, so that only the L2 (6) type scheme exists. When n=
we can presently construct Latin square type L₁(10) association schemes for i=2, 3, and 4 as the best we
can do is construct a pair of mutually orthogonal Latin squares of size 10. When n=12 we can construct
schemes for 2 ≤ i ≤7. In general, when n is a prime or a power of a prime, i can attain the maximum value,
n-1, but when n is a composite nonprime power, then the maximum value that i can attain is dependent
on n and may not be as large as n-1.

The association schemes of Latin square type Li(n) used in this volume are, with but one exception,
standardization association schemes which are tabled in section C of Table II.
2. Constructions. BCS (1954) listed only 20 Latin square type Li(n) designs and for these i=2 or 3.
Electronic computer verification programs uncovered that the plans for designs designated by them as LS15
and LS19 are invalid and that the plan for their LS14 contains a typographical error (the corrected third
20
\n--- PAGE 00000033 ---\nblock being 12 5 14). Design LS15 was corrected and the 19 valid plans have been included in Tables
XII and XIII of the present volume. Even though the square lattice designs of Yates (1936), (1937) may be
classified as D(2) designs based on Latin square association schemes, they were omitted from the BCS (1954)
collection. They are included here because (a) they are valid designs of this classification (b) they are useful
experimental arrangements and hence of potential interest to our readers, and (c) their inclusion brings out,
through their duals, strong combinatorial relationships with many of the SRGD designs. Clatworthy (1956)
expanded the listing of D(2) designs with r, k ≤ 10 based on Latin square type L¡(n) association schemes to
111 parametric specifications by adding to the BCS (1954) listing the designs of the same type with k=2,
by including the lattice designs of Yates, by including n-plicate designs built up from the BCS (1954) designs
and the Yates designs, and by including three newly constructed designs. The present volume contains an
expanded listing beyond Clatworthy's (1956) enumeration. Starting with the latter enumeration, the author
added new designs taken from publications by Bose and Nair (1939) [but omitted from the BCS (1954) list]
P. M. Roy (1954c), Vartak (1955), Shrikhande (1959), Liu (1963), Chang and Liu (1964), Clatworthy (1967)
and five new designs [LS36, LS40, LS44, LS61, LS64] constructed by the author and published here for the
first time. The construction procedures will not be described here, but the reader is referred to the above
references, especially Clatworthy (1967) where very general construction procedures are presented. These
are generalizations of some designs constructed by Chang and Liu (1964), and the generalizations are interest-
ing not only for themselves and a few new resulting designs, but because of the relationships of the present
designs to certain subclasses of GD designs that are brought to light.
The present D(2) designs are strongly related to certain SRGD designs and to D(3) designs.
Of the 146 parametric specifications listed in Tables XII and XIII, there are 56 instances in which the
plans are unique (up to an isomorphism) and 36 cases for which multiple distinct solutions are possible,
leaving 65 cases for which their uniqueness status is unsettled.

\n--- PAGE 00000036 ---\nThe listing of D(2) designs based on cyclic association schemes by BCS (1954) consisted of only 10

cyclic designs have been published by P. M. Roy (1954a), Liu and Chang (1964) and John (1966).
E. Partially Balanced Designs Based on Partial Geometries
1. Partial geometries. A partial geometry (r, k, t) has been defined by Bose (1963) as a system of unde-
fined points and lines and an undefined relation called incidence satisfying the following four axioms:
(i) Any two points are incident with not more than one line.
(ii) Each point is incident with r lines.
(iii) Each line is incident with k points.
(iv) If the point P is not incident with the line l, then there pass through P exactly t lines (t ≥ 1) inter-
secting l.
From this beginning Bose showed that the number v of points and the number b of lines in the partial
geometry
(2.37)
(2.38)
v=k[(r−1) (k−1)+t]/t
b=r[(r-1) (k-1)+t]/t.
He also proved that a necessary condition for the existence of a partial geometry (r, k, t) is that the number
(2.39)
a=rk (r−1) (k−1)/t (k+r-t-1)
is integral.
Another of Bose's basic results is the following: Given a partial geometry (r, k, t) there exists a dual
partial geometry (k, r, t) obtained by interchanging the roles of points and lines in the given geometry.
The partial geometry (r, k, t) has an association scheme wherein two points are first associates if
they are incident with a line of the geometry and they are second associates if they are not incident with
a line.
It then follows from Bose (1963) that the association scheme of a partial geometry (r, k, t) has parameters
n₁ = r(k-1), n₂ = (r− 1) (k − 1) (kt)/t,
(2.40)
(2.41)

r, k, λ₁ = 1, λ2=0 and having other parameters given by (2.37), (2.38), (2.40), and (2.41). Thus the corre-
sponding D(2) design would have parameters

The reader can find interesting relations between Bose's results and publications by Bose and Clatworthy
(1955) which contains the three-parameter family of designs specified by (2.43) and results closely related
to the association scheme of the partial geometry and by Connor and Clatworthy (1954) on which the integral
condition (2.39) on a depends.
The reader should be warned that it has not yet been established that any design of type D(2) having
parameters given by (2.43) is necessarily a partial geometry.
2. Partial geometries which are D(2) designs. It is known (Bose (1963)) that a partial geometry (r, k,
r-1), r≥2 [which is known in certain mathematical areas as a net (r, k) of degree r and order k] is the
same as the lattice design of r replications of Yates (1936) or a D(2) design with blocks of size k=r and r
replications based on the Latin square type L,(r) association scheme. Due to the duality theorem and the
fact that certain SRGD designs and finite Euclidian planes can be constructed from certain L,(n) associ-
ation schemes [Clatworthy (1967)], it follows that many designs we have classified as SRGD designs and
LS designs are also partial geometries.
The partial geometries (2, k, 2), where k=n-1, are known to be triangular designs in which the
blocks are the n rows (columns) of the triangular association scheme with parameter n, i.e., the family
(2.20) of triangular designs of section B of this chapter. The duals of these designs are the well known
unreduced BIB designs of the family
v=n, b= n(n-1)/2,
r=n-1, k = 2, λ = 1.
Thus the unreduced BIB designs with k=2 and certain triangular designs could be classified as partial
geometries.
Certain other designs first constructed by Clatworthy (1954), Bose and Clatworthy (1955), Seiden (1961),
Ray-Chaudhuri (1962), Shrikhande (1965) and Benson (1966) are also known to be partial geometries. [See
the Literature Sources of Table XVI for identification of particular cases with r and k less than 10.]
3. The simple type of D(2) design and the present classification of designs. The design category of BCS
(1954) having one λ equal to zero and labeled "simple" was a convenience classification into which designs
not otherwise classified could be put in 1954. The first three of these designs [Sl. 1, 2 and 3] have long been
known to be invalid and were discarded. Designs Sl. 18, Sl. 19, Sl. 20 and Sl. 26 also had invalid solutions
which were detected by the National Bureau of Standards computer program devised to test the validity
of designs considered for this volume. Designs Sl. 18, Sl. 19, Sl. 20 and Sl. 26 were reconstructed to provide
valid solutions. Except for the discarded Design Sl. 1, all the simple designs of BCS (1954) having λ, = 1
and λ=0 can be classified as partial geometries. Hence these designs and some new partial geometries
[namely, designs PG 1, PG 5, PG 8, PG 16, and PG 17] were put together to form the new classification
of our Tables XVI and XVII for partially balanced designs based on partial geometries. While there are
many partial geometries which are not included in Tables XVI and XVII, many that have r and k less than
or equal to ten are included in other classifications.
-
Those simple designs of BCS (1954) for which A, 2 and λ=0 were constructed in all cases by repli-
cating entire plans of our partial geometries. Since they cannot be classified as partial geometries (for which
A₁ = 1), a new category called Miscellaneous was formed to include these and other new designs for which
the other classifications are not appropriate.
F. Miscellaneous D(2) Designs
The 46 designs of this category are truly miscellaneous. In 17 instances they arise from repetitions of the
whole plan of a partial geometry, thus accounting for those simple type designs of BCS (1954) that were not
accounted for by the partial geometries. There are 7 instances of designs with k=2 from the work of Clat-
worthy (1955) and Liu and Chang (1964). In 5 instances the designs are new [M13, M16, M17, M18, M32],
being published here for the first time. The remaining designs come from scattered publications including
25
\n--- PAGE 00000038 ---\nBose and Nair (1939), Clatworthy (1956), Ray-Chaudhuri (1962), Liu (1963), Liu and Chang (1964), Preece
(1966), Mesner (1967) and Bhagwandas (1968).
Most of the miscellaneous designs are characterized by one zero-valued A but there are 8 instances of
designs that have both A's different from zero. In the latter case the association schemes are of no assistance
in analyzing data arising from these designs. The reader is referred to Rao (1947) who gives general methods
for analysis of incomplete block designs. For those designs having one zero-valued λ the blocks can serve
as association schemes, and then the analysis procedures found in BCS (1954) for the simple type of D(2)
designs are appropriate.
Little is known about uniqueness among the plans for miscellaneous designs. In 21 instances the para-
metric specifications for designs are known to be capable of multiple distinct solutions. Thus the uniqueness
status is an open question for 25 of the 46 parametric specifications.
26
\n--- PAGE 00000039 ---\nChapter III. Analysis of Partially Balanced Designs
1. Introduction. In this chapter, general solutions appropriate for data analysis are given. In section 2
both the intra-block and the combined intra- and inter-block analyses are given for each of the three models
for blocking configurations. Section 3 contains an explanation of the ways the association schemes can be
used to provide easier ways to compute useful quantities for the analysis using hand calculations. Section 4
provides 2 worked out examples as illustrative aids for those who use a desk calculator. Section 5 deals with
the general least squares approach for data analysis, appropriate when calculations are done on an electronic
computer. Finally, section 6 provides an example for use in checking out a computer code that utilizes the
methods of section 5.
Perhaps it should be emphasized that the data being analyzed here were taken from BCS (1954) and
originated at the Agriculture Experiment Station, North Carolina State College. The analyses given in
sections 4 and 6 differ from those of BCS (1954) in numerical computational details and in the provision of
variances for single treatment effects not available in 1954.
2. General analysis of D(2) designs. The general analysis procedure described in this section is a re-
organization of that presented in BCS (1954) which is a refinement of the general analysis of Rao (1947)
for partially balanced designs accomplished by the introduction of certain computational constants c₁,
C2, H, and ▲ for intra-block analyses and additional constants d, and d₂ for the combined intra- and inter-
block analyses of variance. We shall describe both these methods of analysis for each of three models.
Intra-block estimates are obtained from treatment contrasts obtained within the blocks. These arise
through the model commonly known as the fixed effects model in which the additive components representing
treatment effects t; and block effects b; are conceived of as fixed but unknown constants which we may wish
to intercompare.
On the other hand, inter-block estimates arise from the assumption that the block effects are random
variables, stochastically independent of each other and of the errors ej and identically distributed. They
are derived from contrasts among the block averages.
Under normality assumptions the intra-block estimates are independent of the inter-block estimates
and this led to an analysis of D(2) designs in which the estimation of the t is based on a linear combination
of the intra- and inter-block estimates of the ti.
For each of these estimation procedures we shall provide for three different conceptual models depend-
ing upon the nature of the blocking arrangements. These we define briefly.
Model 1 is the case for which there is no provision for grouping of blocks. This model is independent
of whether the b; are considered as fixed effects or random variables.
Model 2 is the model in which the design provides for groupings of the blocks such that within every
group each treatment occurs exactly n(≥ 1) times. The groups we call replication groups. If there are r'
such groups then r=nr'. In the model 2 setup the block effect b; of model 1 is now thought of as b;= pc+x;
where the pe represents a replication effect and the x; is a differential effect of the jth block in its replication
group. The x, is considered to be a fixed but unknown quantity in intra-block analyses, but is conceived of
xj
as a N(0, of) variable in inter-block analyses. The quantity pc is a fixed but unknown constant.
Model 3 is the model appropriate to designs which provide for replication groups orthogonal to the
blocks. Suppose the first e positions in the b blocks give exactly n replications of all treatments, the next
e positions provide exactly n replications, etc. If there are r' replication groups, then r=nr' and k=er'.
These designs provide for a two-way elimination of heterogeneity, i.e., between blocks and between replica-
tion groups orthogonal to blocks. Well-known examples of such designs are the Latin squares [Fisher (1958)]
and the Youden square designs [Youden (1937)]. D(2) and BIB designs having replications orthogonal to
27
\n--- PAGE 00000040 ---\nthe blocks are spoken of as having been Youdenized. The analysis of variance table for Youdenized designs
provides for a replication groups subdivision for which the sums of squares and the degrees of freedom serve
to reduce the corresponding quantities in the "error" line of a model 1 analysis of variance table.
In the "even" numbered Tables VI, VIII, . . ., XVIII of this volume replication groups orthogonal
to the blocks are indicated by vertical rulings in the design plans, while the replication groups of Model 2
are indicated by horizontal rulings.
2.1. Intra-block analyses. In this section we shall present the formulas necessary for the intra-block
analyses and the expressions for the computational constants C1, C2, H, and ▲ and the efficiency factors
E₁, E2, and E. In section 2.2 we specify the formulas for the combined intra- and inter-block analyses.
Let there be v treatments whose effects it is required to compare by using a partially balanced design
with two associate classes. We divide the N(=bk) experimental units to which the treatments are to be
applied into b more or less homogeneous sets called blocks, each block containing k units. The design di-
vides the treatments into b sets, such that each set contains k treatments, and each treatment occurs in r
sets, other conditions for a D(2) design being satisfied. The b sets of treatments are assigned randomly to
the b blocks, and the individual treatments of a set are assigned randomly to the units of the corresponding
block. Let yy be the observed result of applying the ith treatment to a unit in the jth block. We delay consid-
eration of the conceptual composition of the observation y until the three models are discussed separately.
It should be noted that to a given j(j=1, 2, . . ., b) only k different values of i are associated, since the
symbol y or ty is meaningful only when the ith treatment occurs in the jth block.
Denote by T, the total of the observations for the ith treatment, i.e., T; is the sum of the observations
from the r plots containing the ith treatment. Let B; be the sum of the k observations for the jth block. Denote
by Q, the adjusted yield for the ith treatment, where Qi is obtained by subtracting from T; the sum of the block
averages for those blocks in which the ith treatment occurs. Thus, if the ith treatment occurs in blocks num-
bered i, i2, . . ., ir, we have

2.1.2. Model 2: Subsets of blocks as replications. In many designs, it is possible to group the blocks
in such a way, that within every group each treatment is replicated n times. These groups may be called
replication groups. If there are r' such groups, then nr' = r. The experimental material in this case should
be so divided that the blocks to which the treatment sets of the same replication group are assigned, are
relatively more alike. Thus in agronomic experiments, the land may be divided into r' large pieces corres-
ponding to the replication groups, and then each piece is subdivided into b' =b/r' blocks. In actually laying
down the experiment, one makes a three-fold randomization in the assignment of (i) the replication groups to
groups of blocks, (ii) the sets of treatments in a replication group to the individual blocks of the block groups,
and (iii) the treatments of a set to the units in the corresponding block. Suppose that the ith treatment is
applied to a unit in the jth block which occurs in the cth replication group, then we assume the model
(3.21)

2.1.3. Model 3: replication groups orthogonal to blocks. Model 3 in which the design provides for

3. Use of the association schemes in data analysis. Prior to the introduction of the association schemes
by Bose and Shimamoto (1952) the computations in the analysis of data arising from the D(2) type of designs
was rather tedious, especially the evaluation of the v values for S₁ (Qi) for intra-block analyses and the
corresponding values S₁ (Pi) for the combined intra- and inter-block analyses. The association schemes
make possible the analyses in the forms given in section 4.1 and 4.2. We shall describe in this section how
to utilize the association schemes for D(2) type designs to calculate the quantities S₁ (Qi) and S₁ (P₁) for
i=1, 2,..., v.
When digital computers are used, the special features described in this section are of no special interest,
because an analysis based on general linear regression theory (which is in no way dependent on the clas-
sification of designs as group divisible, triangular, etc.) is appropriate. Further, a well designed computer
program will give variances for block effect estimates that are not available from the formulas prepared for
hand computing.
3.1. Group divisible designs. The association schemes for this very large class of D(2) designs are called
groups and are identified as "group (m, n)," meaning an mXn array of treatment symbols 1,2, . . ., v sys-
tematically arranged in m rows (groups) of n distinct treatments per group. The groups listed in Section A
of Table II are standardized and the group (m, n) is of the form

3.2. Triangular designs. The standardized triangular association schemes listed in section B of Table II
are designated by T(n) where n is the parameter defining the scheme. For these association schemes there
are v=n(n−1)/2 treatments where n ≥ 5 is a positive integer. The v treatment symbols 1, 2, 3, . . ., v are
entered successively above the main diagonal of an n× array in which the main diagonal is blanked out
and then the array is completed so as to be symmetrical. Thus the standardized triangular scheme T(5)
has v = 10 treatments arranged in a 5 × 5 array as shown.
1 2 3 4

For the intra-block estimates, ti, of the treatment effects suppose we have already calculated the Qi but
now need the S, (Qi) in order to employ eq (3.10) to calculate (i = 1, 2, . . ., v). Again use the association
scheme, placing the Q; alongside the corresponding treatment symbol i. Then form row and column sums
in the marginal positions. Recall that S₁ (Qi) is the sum of the adjusted treatment yields for all treatments
which are first associates of a given treatment i. Since in a triangular association scheme the first associates of
any treatment i are exactly those treatments that lie in the same rows as treatment i and in the same columns,
the quantity S. (Qi) is the sum of the marginal subtotals of the row and column containing treatment i,
less 2Qi. Thus if n=5 and i=6 in the above scheme, then S, (Q6) will be the second row total plus the fourth
column total less 2Q6. Once the S, (Qi) are calculated they are entered in an auxiliary v-rowed computational
table whose first column consists of the treatment symbols; the second and third columns consist of the
corresponding Qi and S₁ (Qi). One can then use eq (3.10) to compute the fourth column consisting of î¿.
For the combined intra- and inter-block treatment effects estimates, î, one uses the triangular associa-
tion scheme with entries Pi, calculated from (3.26), placed beside treatment i and then calculates the S₁ (Pi)
in precisely the same way as the S₁ (Qi) for i = 1, 2, . . ., v. Knowing the S₁ (Pi), one can set up a v-rowed
auxiliary table with column headings i, Pi, S₁ (Pi) and ↑ and use eq (3.30) to calculate the entries of the
the fourth column.
3.3. D(2) designs based on Latin square types of association schemes. Recall that an L,(n) type associa-
tion scheme, 2<I≤n−1, n> 3, requires 1-2 mutually orthogonal Latin squares for its definition. It
consists of an n×n array of the treatment symbols 1, 2, . . ., v=n² written in the order of the printed page
with the 1-2 sets of Latin square symbols from the 1-2 mutually orthogonal Latin squares superposed on
the square array. The association schemes of type L, (n) used in this volume, unless otherwise specified,
consist of the standardized schemes presented in section C of Table II. Thus in each (i, j)-cell of the as-
sociation scheme one will find one (unique) treatment symbol and I-2 different "kinds" of Latin letters,
37
\n--- PAGE 00000050 ---\none kind belonging to each mutually orthogonal Latin square, provided I>2. If I=2, no Latin square
is needed to define the association scheme of type L2 (n), the scheme now consisting only of the n×n array
of n2 treatment symbols.
For example, the standardized L4(5) type of association scheme utilizes a 5x5 array of the treatment
symbols 1, 2, . . ., 25 on which is superposed two mutually orthogonal Latin squares, the second having
its symbols in bold faced type.

Each treatment i has n₁ = 16 first associates and these consist of the treatments which occur with it in the
same row, or in the same column, or correspond to the same letter from the first Latin square or correspond
to the same letter from the second Latin square. Thus the first associates of treatment 8 are:
6, 7, 9, 10; 3, 13, 18, 23; 2, 14, 20, 21; 4, 12, 16, 25.
The second associates of treatment i will be those treatments which are not its first associates. Thus the
n2=8 second associates of treatment 8 are 1, 5; 11, 15; 17, 19; 22, 24.
Further, any pair of treatments which are first associates will appear together in A, blocks and any pair
which are second associates will appear together in A₂ blocks of any design which is based on the L, (n)
type of association scheme.

3.4. D(2) designs based on cyclic association schemes. In section D of Table II association schemes of
cyclic type are given for five values of v, namely v=5, 13, 17, 19, 37. These are standardized in the form
C(v: X1, X2,...,xn)
from which one obtains the n₁ first associates of treatment i (i=1, 2, . . ., v) by adding i to the numbers
X1, X2,. . ., xn, and reducing the sums modulo v whenever they exceed v. Any treatment that is not a first
associate of treatment i is its second associate.
For example, the standard association scheme for v=5 treatments is given as C(5: 2, 3). The first
associates of treatment i are shown in the table.

For intra-block estimates the quantities Q are listed in the cyclic association scheme alongside the
treatment symbols for first associates of each treatment i (i = 1, 2, . . ., v). Row totals are then formed and
they are the S₁(Qi). For computation of S₁ (Pi) we substitute the P; for the Qi and then the row totals give
S₁(Pi).
3.5. D(2) designs based on partial geometries. For the partial geometries A₁ = 1 and λ=0 always, so
that the experimental plan itself can serve as an association scheme. There will be r blocks that contain
treatment i and those r(k-1) other treatments in the blocks with i are its n₁ first associates. All other
treatments (which never appear in a block with treatment i) are its n₂ second associates.
Also among the type of D(2) designs called miscellaneous nearly all designs have A₁ = c, c a positive
integer, and λ20 or A₁ = 0 and λ= c. Hence, what is said here about use of the blocks of the design as an
association scheme, applies also to nearly all miscellaneous designs.
=
The arrangement of the auxiliary computation tables for desk calculator analysis of designs of miscel-
laneous type D(2) designs based on partial geometries will not be described here. The interested reader is
referred to the statistical analysis section for "simple partially balanced incomplete block designs" in
Chapter III of BCS (1954) for the appropriate suggestions for analyses of these designs [which were previously
classified as "simple"].
4. Illustrative examples as guides for analyses using desk calculators. Whenever electronic computers
are available, their use in analyses of the D(2) type of incomplete block design is highly recommended. How-
39
\n--- PAGE 00000052 ---\never, the calculations proceed quite routinely and easily using a desk calculator, especially for the smaller
designs and for larger designs whenever the association schemes are utilized to simplify calculations as
indicated in section 3. Data analyses are given here for two types of designs, the group divisible design with
model 1 structure and the triangular design with replications orthogonal to the blocks (with a model 3
structure).

design, R114, is then found in Tables VIII and IX where the parameters, computational constants, efficiency
factors and plan are given. The association scheme (or groups) is identified as groups (5, 3) of section A,

The experimental plan given in Table 7 is that of R114 Table IX after subsets of four experimental units
have been randomly assigned to the block symbols (j) and the treatments of the blocks have been randomly
assigned to the experimental units within the blocks. The details of the analysis are subdivided into 5 steps.
Step. 1. Preparation of the experimental plan and treatment plan and associated calculations. The
experimental plan, Table 7, shows the block number in the first column. In the second column opposite
each block number (and in the same row) are given the treatments appearing in this block, and below each
treatment number is shown the corresponding observed yield. For example, the experimental unit carrying
the treatment 9 in block (1) gave a yield of 2.5. The third column of the experimental plan shows the block
totals, B,, obtained by adding the yields of all the experimental units in the block. Thus B₁ = 9.5 is obtained
by adding the four yields 2.4, 2.5, 2.6, 2.0. The last column of the experimental plan gives the block average,
B/k, obtained by dividing the corresponding item in the previous column by k. The total of the third column
gives the grand total, G, of all the observations. This is 164.0 in Table 7. Finally below the last column is
entered the grand mean, G/N, for later use (N= vrbk being the total number of experimental units).
