come from Table XI. The plan has been used for two-way elimination of heterogeneity and the blocks (rows)
and positions within blocks (columns) have been randomized separately as instructed for Model 3 designs
wherein we assumed the observation y₁j to be a linear combination
(3.45)
Yij=g+ti + pc + bj+€ij.
In the intra-block analysis we assume that the overall mean, g, the treatment effects, ti (i=1,2, . . ., 10),
the block effects, b; (j=1,2, . . ., 10), and the effects of positions within blocks, pc (c=1,2,3,4), are
fixed, but unknown quantities and the ej are random variables with means 0 and constant variance σ².
For the computation of the analysis of variance, the various estimates of effects ti, bj, and pc and their vari-
ances, no further distributional assumptions about the e₁j's are necessary, but once inferences of probabil-
istic nature are made we shall assume the e's are normally distributed. Recall further that the only subscript
combinations ij that are relevant are for cases for which the treatment labeled i occurs in the block labeled j.
The data for this example were taken from page 45 of BCS (1954) and their source is described below
Table 16.
It is convenient to divide the computation into a number of steps.
Step 1. Preparation of the experimental and treatment allocation plans. The experimental plan, Table
16, shows the block number in the first column. In the second column opposite each block number (and
in the same row) are given the treatments appearing in this block, and below each treatment number is
shown the corresponding observed yield. The third column of the experimental plan shows the block totals,
B₁, obtained by adding the yields of all the plots in the block, (j). The last column of the field plan gives
the block average, Bj/k, obtained by dividing the corresponding item in the previous column by k. It should
be noted that each subcolumn of column (2) gives a complete replication, and the totals of the yields in
these subcolumns given in the last row of Table 16 are replication totals. The total of the third column gives
the grand total, G, of all the observations. Finally below the last column is entered the grand mean, G/N,
for later use (N= vr=bk, being the total number of plots).

The observations have been obtained by superposing the design on a part of a blank experiment planted uniformly to Mexican
87 variety of cotton, located on the Upper Coastal Plain Branch Experiment Station, Rocky Mount, North Carolina. This cotton was
grown during 1941. The stand was fairly uniform and all rows received the same fertilizer and cultural treatments. Thirty-six rows, 300
feet long were harvested in sections 25 feet in length. A cord was stretched across the field perpendicular to the rows at intervals of 25
feet. The cotton from each 25 foot plot was harvested, weighed, and the weight recorded separately. These weights varied from 0.93 to
3.25 pounds per plot, with a mean of 2.21 ±.20 pounds. A total of 432 plots were harvested in this manner.
The experimental plan, as it emerged after randomization, was superposed on a randomly selected part of the blank experiment
described above, the blocks being four consecutive plots of the same row, and the replications running perpendicular to the rows over
ten rows.
The treatment allocation plan, Table 17 shows the treatment number in the first column. In the second
column opposite each treatment number (and in the same row) are given the blocks in which this treatment
appears, and below each block number is shown the corresponding observed yield. In the next row of the
second column immediately below the observed yields are shown the block averages (taken from the final
column of the experimental plan, Table 16). The third column gives the treatment total, Ti, and the adjust-
ment, Ti-Qi. The value of Ti is obtained by adding the yields in the second row of the second column
opposite the treatment i. Thus
T₁ = 2.31+2.86+1.65+2.58=9.40,
and the value of T-Qi is obtained by adding the block averages in the third row of the second column
opposite the treatment i. Thus
T₁-Q₁=2.3825 +2.7375 +1.8975+2.6525=9.6700.

5. Computer analysis. In this section the analysis of partially balanced block designs is given in a
general form suitable for programming an electronic computer. The three models of section 2 are discussed
separately in subsections, treating separately the case where the blocks are regarded as parameters (intra-
block analysis) and the case where the block values are regarded as random variables (inter-block analysis).
Partitioned matrices are used to show ways to reduce the size of matrices to be inverted. In the case of one
or more missing observations it may be possible to solve by use of linear regression techniques on the
observation equations.
In this section and in section 6, matrices and vectors are denoted by bold face letters; elements of
matrices are denoted by the corresponding lower case letters, e.g., C= (cij). Otherwise, notation is con-
sistent with the foregoing sections unless specially defined in these sections.
5.1. Intra-block analysis.
5.1.1. Model 1: No replication grouping. The expected value and variance of the observation on the
ith treatment in the jth block are
E(yij) = g+ti + bj
V(yij) = σ².
The assignment of treatments to blocks can be represented by the incidence matrix, N, of the design where
N is the v by b matrix which has the value 1 in the (i, j)th cell if treatment i occurs in block j and zero other-
wise. For example, for the v=4, b=8, r=4, k=2 design, R1, the incidence matrix is
N=
=
1
0 0 1

Parameter estimates
The estimates of the parameters of the design and their variances are as follows:
(a) grand mean
G
bk
V(8) = = k
1
σ-2
*The standard practice of introducing a grand mean, and having to then impose the restraints that block and treatment effects sum to zero is convenient and causes no confusion
when only contrasts among treatments or among blocks are to be studied. When other functions of the parameters are of interest it is necessary to formulate the statistical model
for one's experiment in terms of the parameters g, t₁, and b,; otherwise, the block estimate and variances and covariances may be incorrectly interpreted. An example may point
up the problem. Consider a case where the blocks are different environmental conditions and the treatments are methods, one of which, t₁, is regarded as the standard method.
One would wish to estimate the parameters for "conditions" as
and for methods one would have
8++ b, (block values under standard method t₁)
g+t+b (treatment values under standard conditions)
where the conditions of the first block, b₁, are regarded as standard.
There is also a question of the interpretation of the grand mean. Note that in a repetition of the design with the same blocks but with say one treatment changed to produce
a difference in effect of A, then the effect of the restraint is to change the expected value of g by A/v. For this reason, it is difficult to attach physical meaning to the parameter g.

Analysis of variance
The analysis of variance can be computed as shown in Table 1. It should be noted, however, that the
error sum of squares can easily be calculated directly in the computer from the formula
S²=Σ(yu-ŷü)².

5.1.2. Model 2: Groups of blocks form complete replications. If the blocks are grouped in such a way
that within each of r' groups each treatment is replicated n times then the analysis is slightly modified.
The basic structure of the experiment may be expressed as
E(yijc)=g+ti + pc + bj

5.2.2. Model 2: Groups of blocks form complete replications. If groups of blocks have associated with
them fixed effects, pc, and if the blocks that make up the group contain n complete replications, then the
analysis can be modified quite simply. The structure of the experiment is represented by the observational
equation
Vijc―ti + Pc+nj + Eijc
where the replication parameters, pc, sum up to zero.
In the analysis of variance, the block sum of squares is partitioned as shown in section 5.1.2. The ex-
pected values of the sums of squares are
E(S2) = (b-r') o² + (N −v-kr' + k) of
E (S²) = (N − v − b + 1) σ²
from which one solves for o² and σ by using the observed values in place of the expected values. The
values for w = 1/σ² and w' = 1/(σ² + ko?) are then computed and used as in the analysis given in section
5.2.1.

5.2.3. Replication groups orthogonal to blocks (Youdenized). When the treatments are arranged in
complete replications by rows of the design as described in section 5.1.3., then the model becomes
Yijcti+ Pc + Nj + Eijc.
As before, because the replications are orthogonal to blocks and treatments, the only change has to do with
the partitioning of the error estimate. This is carried out exactly as in the intra-block analysis.

\n--- PAGE 00000081 ---\n6. Example. The example given here is the same as the second example given in section 4. The plan
used is the triangular design T33 of Table XI with parameters v = 10, r=4, k=4, b = 10. Each position
within blocks provides a replication (as in Model 3). The plan and data are repeated in Table 25 for complete-
ness, along with the block totals.
It should be pointed out that the purpose of this section is not to show how to write a computer pro-
gram for the analysis of partially balanced designs, but to provide numerical results to aid in checking
intermediate (and final) results of such a program. Note also that some of the intermediate results given
in this section may not agree with their counterparts in section 4.2. For an additional example done in the
style of this section, see the example given in John [1972].

\n--- PAGE 00000091 ---\nTable I. Glossary and Notation
a. General
balanced incomplete block design- An arrangement of v treatments in b blocks, each of size k, such that (1) no treatment occurs
more than once in any block, (2) each treatment occurs in r blocks, and (3) such that every pair of treatments occurs in exactly
A blocks. Such a design may be denoted by BIBD, by BIB design, by D(1) or by its parameters expressed as an ordered pentuple
(v, b, r, k, λ). For BIB designs it is necessary (but not sufficient) that vr= = bk, λ(v − 1) = r(k − 1), k < v, d < r < b, v ≤ b (or
rk).
-
BIBD (v, b, r, k, λ) – A balanced incomplete block design whose parameters are v, b, r, k, λ. When the design is symmetric (v=b)
then the notation is abbreviated to "BIBD (v, k, λ)."
v-the number of treatments in a design
b-the number of blocks in a design
r-the number of replications; the number of blocks in which each treatment appears
k-the number of treatments contained in a block
A-in a balanced incomplete block design A is the number of blocks in which a pair of treatments occurs.
D(m), m ≥ 2, and integral - An abbreviation for "a partially balanced incomplete block design with m associate classes." Example:
"Dual: D(3)" means that the dual of the design being enumerated is a partially balanced design with three associate classes,
but that identification details are omitted.
BCS (1954)- A much-used abbreviation for reference to the original publication.
R. C. Bose, W. H. Clatworthy and S. S. Shrikhande, Tables of Partially Balanced Designs with Two Associate Classes, North
Carolina Agricultural Experiment Station, Technical Bulletin, No. 107, 1954.
plan or solution - An arrangement of treatments in blocks according to the specifications provided by the parameters is called a
plan or a solution. Sometimes only one combinationally distinct (unique) solution is possible for a set of parameters; for other
sets of parameters it may be possible to construct more than one combinationally distinct (nonunique) plan.
the true n-plicate (for n=2, duplicate) solution for the BIB design (v, bn, rn, k, nλ) — A description of a particular solution (or plan)
for BIB design with the recorded parameters. To construct it one would construct a plan for the BIB design with parameters
( v, b, r, k, λ), and then repeat this plan n times (n=2, 3, . . .) to obtain the desired plan for the referenced design.
b. Uniqueness and Nonuniqueness
isomorphic - Two designs are said to be isomorphic if by a 1-1 transformation of the treatments of the plan of one of the designs,
its blocks can be brought into coincidence with the blocks of the other, the order of treatments within blocks being of no con-
sequence.
Clearly, for two partially balanced designs with two associate classes to be isomorphic it is necessary that they have the same
sets of parameters, and the transformation of treatments must provide identical association schemes for the designs.
nonisomorphic-Two designs which have the same set of parameters are nonisomorphic (nonunique, distinct, combinationally
distinct) if they are not isomorphic.
unique- A design is said to be unique if all possible solutions or plans for that design can be put into isomorphism with any one
of them.
nonunique-See nonisomorphic.
c. Resolvability
resolvable - A plan or solution for a design is said to be resolvable if its blocks can be divided into subsets each of which contains
every treatment exactly once. Clearly, for resolvable designs it is necessary that b/r=v/k an integer.
a-resolvable (a= 1, 2, . . . or c <r) – A design whose blocks can be divided into subsets such that each subset contains every
treatment precisely a times where a is an integer less than r, is called an a-resolvable design or is said to have an a-resolvable
plan (or solution). Clearly, r/a must be an integer. A 1-resolvable design is a resolvable design.
79
\n--- PAGE 00000092 ---\nnonresolvable-A design that is not resolvable is said to be nonresolvable (i.e., it is not one-resolvable ). However, a nonresolvahe
design may be a-resolvable where 2 < a <r.
affine resolvable design – A resolvable design for which every block intersects all the blocks outside its own replication in a constant
number of treatments is called affine resolvable. For such designs it is necessary that k²/v be integral.
d. Complementary designs
complementary design-The complement of a design D is a design D* such that D* has the same number of treatments v and the
same number of blocks b as the parent design D, and whose jth block consists of precisely those treatments which do not
occur in the jth block (j = 1, 2, . . ., b) of the parent design D.
-
tabular entry for complementary designs, “Design AB.xyz” – Whenever the complementary design of a particular listed design
is identified as Design AB.xyz it is meant that the result of the complementation operation can be placed into isomorphism with
the design identified as Design AB.xyz.
a degenerate design - An expression used to partially describe the complement of a particular recorded design (plan) when each
treatment appears only r=1 time in the complementary design. Obviously this degenerate complementary design is unique
(up to an isomorphism) so that its complement (the parent design) has a unique solution (up to an isomorphism).
connected design — A design is a connected design if every block and every treatment of the design are connected to every other block
and treatment. Two treatments, two blocks or a block and a treatment are said to be connected if it is possible to pass from
one to the other by means of a chain consisting alternately of blocks and treatments such that any two consecutive members
of the chain (i.e., a treatment and a block) are such that the treatment occurs in the block.
disconnected design - A design is said to be disconnected if it breaks up into connected parts such that a block or a treatment of
one part is unconnected with a block or a treatment of another part.
NL, r=c- An expression used to partially describe the complement of a particular design. It means that the complementary design
is not listed in the present enumeration but that it is a design of the same type and it has r=c replications.
NL, k=c-An expression used to partially describe the complement of a particular listed design. It means that the complementary
design is not listed in the enumeration of the present publication but that it is a design of the same type whose block size is
k = c.
self complementary design- A design D is said to be self complementary if its complementary design D* has precisely the same
parameters as the parent design D. The designs D and D* may or may not be isomorphic. For a design to be self complementary,
it is clearly necessary that v=2k (b=2r).
SC-self complementary
truly self complementary-If a design D is self complementary (SC) and if D and its complementary design are also isomorphic, we
say design D is truly self complementary (TSC). A TSC design is always a SC design but a SC design may not be a TSC design.
TSC-truly self complementary.
e. Dual designs
dual design-The dual (or inverse) of a design D is a design D' whose treatments are the blocks of design D and whose blocks are
the treatments of design D. If design D has parameters (v, b, r, k, . . .) then its dual D' has parameters (v', b', r', k', . . .)
= (b, v, k, i r, .). If N = (nij) is the v× b incidence matrix of the design D then the incidence matrix of design D' is the b×v
matrix N', where N' is the transpose of N.
.
-
tabular entry for dual designs, “Design AB.xyz” – Whenever the dual design of a particular solution (or plan) is identified as Design
AB.xyz, it is meant that the inversion produces a design which can be set into an isomorphism with the recorded solution for
Design AB.xyz. Since the dual (or inverse design) depends upon the order in which the blocks are arranged prior to inversion,
the dual design can assume many different appearances, all of which can be placed into isomorphism with any one of the
inversions.
irregular - A dual of the design D is said to be irregular when it is not a D(1) or a partially balanced incomplete design of m associate
classes (denoted by D(m)) where m ≥ 2. Clearly, if the design D possesses two or more distinct types of block structure, its
dual will be irregular.
.
..
not a D(x), x= 1, 2,
t, t an integer - It may be quite difficult to specifically identify the type of design that the dual design
actually is. In the difficult cases it may be easy to specify some design types that the dual is not. “Not a D(1) means that the dual
design is not a balanced incomplete block design, and "not a D(x), x=2, . . t" means that the dual design is not a partially
balanced design with x associate classes (x = 2, or 3, . . ., or t).
self dual- A design D is said to be self dual if its dual design D' has the same parameters as design D. Clearly, for a design to be
self dual it is necessary that v=b and r=k. It is not necessary that the dual D' of a design D be such that D is isomorphic to D'.
80
\n--- PAGE 00000093 ---\nSD-self dual
f.
-
truly self dual – A design D which is self dual and whose dual D' is isomorphic to D is a truly self dual (TSD) design. A TSD design
is always a SD design, but a SD design may not be a TSD design.
TSD-truly self dual.
truly self-dual, Hoffman (1963), and Clatworthy (1971a) - By application of a theorem of Hoffman, published in 1963, it may be
shown that the design being referenced has as its dual a design with the same parameters (i.e., it is self dual); by the work of
Clatworthy it is established that the design is truly self dual.
a D(c), Clatworthy (1956) – A reference to the dual of the particular recorded design. The dual design is a partially balanced design
with c associate classes, c being an integer> 3. This result is known as a consequence of a theorem by Clatworthy published in
1956.
Association schemes
association scheme-For partially balanced designs with two associate classes, the association scheme is an array of the treat-
ment symbols which provides concise and complete information on the first and second associates of any treatment. For
convenience and economy most of the designs in this publication have had their treatment symbols labeled so as to put their
association schemes into standardized forms and these are specified in Table II.
Groups: (x, y)—The particular design construction having this recorded entry is a design based on a group divisible association
scheme with n = y treatments in each of m = x distinct groups. The symbol (m, n) refers to a particular standardized associa-
tion scheme recorded in Table II.
T(n), n=5, 6, 7, -A symbol used to identify a standardized association scheme of the triangular type with parameter n.
The standardized triangular association schemes denoted by T(n) are the appropriate ones for the associated plans and
they are recorded in Table II.
Li(n)-A symbol used to denote the standardized form of the Latin square type of association scheme with parameter n and
i(≥2) constraints. The various Latin square types of association schemes in standardized forms and denoted by L¡(n) are
recorded in Table II. When in or n + 1 the association scheme is of the group divisible type.
L* (4)—A symbol used to identify a Latin square type association scheme with i = 3 constraints and parameter n = 4 which is
distinct from the one denoted by L3 (4).
Association scheme: a pseudo Latin square type-An association scheme whose parameters are those of the Latin square type
but whose treatment relations are not describable by a Latin square type of association scheme.
NLÎ(y), y=2, 3,
Mesner (1967).
•
.
or 10-A symbol used to denote the negative Latin square type of association scheme as developed by
C(x), where x = 5, 13, 17, 29, or 37-This symbol C(x) identifies the particular association scheme of cyclic type appropriate to
the particular design being enumerated. The cyclic association schemes denoted by C(x) are provided in Table II.
C(13: 1, 3, 4, 9, 10, 12); nonstandard – A specification of a particular association scheme of cyclic type. To identify the six first
associates of the treatment labeled i, add i to each of the numbers 1, 3, 4, 9, 10, 12, reducing the results modulo 13.
Association Scheme; not identified-The association scheme is of the general type indicated by the enumeration, but the particular
configuration of treatments in the association scheme has not been identified.
Association Scheme: no information given-For designs of the class D(2) based on partial geometries (i.e., designs with reference
numbers PGxy) no association scheme is needed as the blocks themselves serve as an association scheme since A₁ = 0,
A₂ = 1. The same situation holds for designs of the Miscellaneous classification where no information is recorded.
g. Plans
Plan: Repeat the plan of AB.xyz c times-The particular plan being enumerated may be constructed by repeating the plan of
Design AB.xyz c times. "Duplicate AB.xyz” means to repeat the blocks of Design AB.xyz twice.
Invert Design AB.xyz—To invert a design is to dualize it, i.e., to interchange the roles of treatments and blocks.
h.
Literature references
81
\n--- PAGE 00000094 ---\nlisted references in brackets-Whenever listed references are contained in brackets, [], in Tables VI, VIII, X, . it is intended
to convey the assertion that the particular plan, solution or construction of the author referred to is isomorphic with the con-
struction provided by the author listed immediately to the left of the brackets. For example, the reference "Bose and Nair
(1939) [Clatworthy (1967), John (1966)], Freeman (1957)" would mean that the solutions published by Clatworthy (1967) and
John (1966) can be put into isomorphism with the solution published by Bose and Nair (1939) but that the solution of Freeman
(1957) is possibly distinct.
Design Sl.xy-An identifying symbol for designs of the "simple" design classification enumerated in the publication by Bose,
Clatworthy and Shrikhande (1954).

D. CYCLIC TYPE ASSOCIATION SCHEMES, C(n).
D. CYCLIC TYPE ASSOCIATION SCHEMES
RULE. TO DETERMINE THe first associates of TREATMENT I
(I=12, . . ., V) ADD I TO THE NUMBERS X1, X2, . . ., XN1, IN
C(V:X1, X2, . . ., XN1) AND REDUCE THE SUMS MODULO V.
THEN ANY TREATMENT THAT IS NOT A FIRST ASSOCIATE
OF TREATMENT I IS ITS SECOND ASSOCIATE.
C(37): C(37:1,3,4,7,9,10,11,12,16,21,25,26,27,28,30,33,34,36)
DESIGN C20

Design SR110:v = 90,
0, c₂
m
10,
r = 9,
n= 9,
=
k = 10,
λ₂
b= 81
0, A2
= 1
1/9, H= 171/10, A = 729/10
E, .90, E₂ = .91, E= .91
Solution:
Nonunique; nonresolvable, Bose and
Connor (1952)
Complement: Not listed, r = 72
Dual: The BIB design (81,90,10,9,1), Roy and
Laha (1956).
Groups: (10,9)
Plan:
To construct the b = 81 blocks of this
design one can start with a solution for
the symmetrical balanced incomplete block
design (v, b, r, k, λ) = (91, 91, 10, 10, 1). It
is known [see Marshall Hall, Jr., J. Dean
Swift and Raymond Killgrove (1959)]
that there exist four solutions for this
design, but one solution may be found in
T. Kitagwa and M. Mitome (1955). From
this design select for deletion 10 blocks
containing a common treatment (say the
treatment labeled 91). Upon removal of
the 10 chosen blocks, strike out the
common treatment (91) and use the 10
subsets so obtained as the 'groups' for
the desired GD design. The set of 81
residual blocks constitute a solution for
design SR110. The groups will not be in
the standardized form, and therefore,
relabeling of treatments both in the plan
and in the groups would be required if
standardization is desired.
Since there exist 4 distinct solutions
for the SBIB design and since the deletion
of blocks from each of them can be done in
91 ways, it should be simple, though
tedious, to construct several independent
plans for this GD design.

Note: There exist many distinct solutions.
The plan may be constructed by taking any
solution for the BIB design (15,35,7,3,1)
which possesses five blocks forming a
replication, annexing this replication to
the BIB design, and finally relabeling
the treatments in standard form so that
the blocks of the replication constitute
the groups.
The BIB design is known to
possess 80 distinct solutions, 7 of
which are resolvable. See Marshall Hall,
Jr. and J. D. Swift (1955) and Cole (1922).

Plan: To the 8 replications of design R82,
Solution 1, add two new replications

twice.
Note: By use of the 7 distinct resolvable
solutions available for the BIB design
(15,35,7,3,1) and recorded by Cole (1922)
one can choose any complete replication
and annex it x times to the BIB design to
obtain a solution for the regular group
divisible design with parameters
v = 15, b= 35+ 5x, 5, λ,
r = 7 + x, k 3, n = 3, λ₂
In this way one can construct many
distinct resolvable solutions for the
regular group divisible design above.

Blocks of three treatments which are
=
1, λη
=
0)
mutual first associates (A,
are formed by annexing to every pair of
treatments occurring in the same row
(column) of the triangular association
scheme a third treatment which lies at
the intersection of the column containing
one of the treatments and the row contain-
ing the other. The set of all distinct
triples so formed constitutes the plan.
This is the method used to construct the
plans for designs T9, Solution 1, T14,
T20, T23, T24, T25, T26, and T27.

Dual: Self dual, Hoffman (1963)
Association Scheme: Unspecified, but triangular
with parameter n = 10.
Plan:
S
=
A procedure exists for a construction of
the plan for this design but the details
are too involved for hand work. A
computer would be useful for identifying
the associate relations among the 495
distinct treatment-pairs. We shall be
content to describe the only known method
of construction. One starts with a
solution for the symmetric BIB design Ds
whose parameters are (v, b, r, k, λ)
(56,56,11,11,2), a solution having been
first found by Marshall Hall, Jr. (1968).
From Ds one derives an unsymmetrical
design Ď, with parameters (v, b, r, k, λ)
(45,55,11,9,2) by deleting one block
and all its treatments. One then
constructs a design D, from D, by
dualizing D, (renaming treatments blocks
and blocks, treatments). The inverted
design D is known to be a PBIB(2) from
a theorem due to Shrikhande (1952); and
from the work of Connor (1958), Shrikhande
(1959), Chang (1959,1960), and Hoffman
(1960) it is known to be based on a
triangular association scheme. Having
obtained design D, which has parameters
v = 55, b
45, n,= 18, n
=
r =
2
=
9, k = 11, λ,

21
one can apply Lemma 1 of E. Seiden (1963)
to delete from D, all the treatments of
any row (column) of the triangular
association scheme associated with design
D. The effect of this deletion is to
remove 2 treatments from every block of
D and to totally remove 11 treatments
(without affecting any of the associate
relations among the residual treatments).
The resulting residual array provides a
plan for the present triangular design in
which the treatment symbols would not

described by Clatworthy (1968) for an
entire collection of four families of

Solution: Unique; 4-resolvable
Complement: Not listed, r = 90
Dual: Not a D(x), x = 1, 2, 3
Association Scheme: L₂(7)
Plan: Utilize the BIB design (7,7,4,4,2), whose
unique solution is provided by developing
the initial block 1 2 3 5 modulo 7, as
a model for constructing BIB designs from
each of the rows and from each of the
columns of the L,(7) association scheme.
The 98 blocks so obtained constitute a
plan for the present design. With careful
placement of treatments within blocks the
plan becomes 4-resolvable with 2 replica-
tions for each position within blocks.

Solution:
Nonresolvable
Complement: Not listed, r
=
40
Dual: Self dual, Archbold and Johnson (1956)
Association Scheme: L₂(7)
Plan: To construct the plan first cyclically
develop 7 initial blocks from the general-
ized initial block
=
7i+ 1, 7i+ 2, 7i + 4, 7(i + 1) + 1,
7(i + 1) + 2, 7(i + 1) + 4, 7(i + 3) + 1,
7(i + 3) + 2, 7(i + 3) + 4, where i 0,
1, 2,..., 6 and (i + z) is reduced modulo 7.
Then cyclically develop these seven initial
blocks using cycles of length 7 and cycl-
ing within subsets of treatments 1-7, 8-14,
15-21, 22-28, 29-35, 36-42, and 43-49.
Each position within blocks contains a
replication.

parameters but a nonisomorphic plan, and

128/3
Association Scheme: A pseudo Latin square type;
blocks provide the scheme.
Plan: Start with design LS17, Solution 2 and
retain its blocks but interchange the
names of the associate classes.
We now
have a design with the parameters for a
Latin square type L₂(4) design.
However, it is now impossible to describe
the association relations by means of a
4 x 4 array (as may be seen by considering
treatments 1, 2, 5, and 6 only and their
placement in the array). Hence the design
is not a Latin square type but has a
pseudo Latin square association scheme
which is determined from the blocks.

Solution:
Nonunique; resolvable
Complement: Truly self complementary
Dual: Design SR100, Solution 2
Association Scheme: Pseudo Latin square type;
blocks provide the scheme
Plan: Start with design LS99, Solution 2 and
interchange the names of its associate
classes but retain its blocks intact.
The resulting design has parameters appro-
priate for an L₂(4) association scheme,
but a 4x4 array of treatment symbols is
inadequate for a specification of the
association relations between the treat-
ments. Hence the design is classified as
miscellaneous and its association scheme
is called a pseudo Latin square type
association scheme. It is nonisomorphic
with design LS100.

15. SUPPLEMENTARY NOTES
16. ABSTRACT (A 200-word or less factual summary of most significant information. If document includes a significant
bibliography or literature survey, mention it here.)
This book is a revised and vastly expanded version of the monograph R. C. Bose,
W. H. Clatworthy and S. S. Shrikhande, Tables of Partially Balanced Designs With Two
Associate Classes, Tech. Bul. No. 107 of the North Carolina Agricultural Experiment
Station, Raleigh, North Carolina, 1954.
Approximately 950 combinatorially distinct designs are given, which represents a
doubling of the number of parametric specifications for designs of the original
publication. The introduction includes special instructions to aid analysis using hand
calculations and a section on the general least squares approach for use when the
analysis is to be done on an electronic computer.
For those interested in the construction of the designs, information is provided
on methods of construction and extensive references, including original sources for
the designs, are given. A tabulation of known information about uniqueness,
resolvability and the nature of the corresponding dual and complementary designs is
also included for each design.
Analysis of PBIB designs; Experiment designs; Finite fields; Finite geometries;
17. KEY WORDS (Alphabetical order, separated by semicolons) Finite projective planes; Incidence matrices;
Incomplete block designs, partially balanced; Mathematics; Partially balanced incomplete
block designs; PBIB designs; Statistics; Tables of PBIB designs;

NBS TECHNICAL PUBLICATIONS
PERIODICALS
JOURNAL OF RESEARCH reports National
Bureau of Standards research and development in
physics, mathematics, and chemistry. Comprehensive
scientific papers give complete details of the work,
including laboratory data, experimental procedures,
and theoretical and mathematical analyses. Illustrated
with photographs, drawings, and charts. Includes
listings of other NBS papers as issued.
Published in two sections, available separately:
• Physics and Chemistry (Section A)
Papers of interest primarily to scientists working in
these fields. This section covers a broad range of
physical and chemical research, with major emphasis
on standards of physical measurement, fundamental
constants, and properties of matter. Issued six times
a year. Annual subscription: Domestic, $17.00; For-
eign, $21.25.
• Mathematical Sciences (Section B)
Studies and compilations designed mainly for the
mathematician and theoretical physicist. Topics in
mathematical statistics, theory of experiment design,
numerical analysis, theoretical physics and chemistry,
logical design and programming of computers and
computer systems. Short numerical tables. Issued quar-
terly. Annual subscription: Domestic, $9.00; Foreign,
$11.25.
TECHNICAL NEWS BULLETIN
The best single source of information concerning the
Bureau's measurement, research, developmental, co-
operative, and publication activities, this monthly
publication is designed for the industry-oriented
individual whose daily work involves intimate contact
with science and technology-for engineers, chemists,
physicists, research managers, product-development
managers, and company executives. Includes listing of
all NBS papers as issued. Annual subscription: Do-
mestic, $6.50; Foreign, $8.25.
NONPERIODICALS
Applied Mathematics Series. Mathematical tables,
manuals, and studies.
Building Science Series. Research results, test
methods, and performance criteria of building ma-
terials, components, systems, and structures.
Handbooks. Recommended codes of engineering
and industrial practice (including safety codes) de-
veloped in cooperation with interested industries,
professional organizations, and regulatory bodies.
Special Publications. Proceedings of NBS confer-
ences, bibliographies, annual reports, wall charts,
pamphlets, etc.
Monographs. Major contributions to the technical
literature on various subjects related to the Bureau's
scientific and technical activities.
National Standard Reference Data Series.
NSRDS provides quantitative data on the physical
and chemical properties of materials, compiled from
the world's literature and critically evaluated.
Product Standards. Provide requirements for sizes,
types, quality, and methods for testing various indus-
trial products., These standards are developed co-
operatively with interested Government and industry
groups and provide the basis for common understand-
ing of product characteristics for both buyers and
sellers. Their use is voluntary.
Technical Notes. This series consists of communi-
cations and reports (covering both other-agency and
NBS-sponsored work) of limited or transitory interest.
Federal Information Processing Standards
Publications. This series is the official publication
within the Federal Government for information on
standards adopted and promulgated under the Public
Law 89-306, and Bureau of the Budget Circular A-86
entitled, Standardization of Data Elements and Codes
in Data Systems.
Consumer Information Series. Practical informa-
tion, based on NBS research and experience, cover-
ing areas of interest to the consumer. Easily under-
standable language and illustrations provide useful
background knowledge for shopping in today's tech-
nological marketplace.
